Refutation of Two Eternal States: Core Contradiction and First Consequence
Maturidi refutes the view that an eternal thing can have two opposite states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that an eternal thing can change from one state to another is rationally contradictory, and what that implies for the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
An eternal thing cannot change states without losing its eternality.

Imam Maturidi begins by exposing the logical flaw in the claim that the world has two eternally opposite states. He shows that if an eternal thing were to change from one state to another, the cause of its existence would be removed, leading to the absurdity that the pre-eternal becomes temporal. This reasoning forces the conclusion that the world must have come into existence after non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how Maturidi states the contradiction at the outset and follows it with a logical argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key steps
Break down the argument: the view ascribes one state to the thing as pre-eternal, then that state departs without the thing itself departing. What does that entail?
3
Understand the first consequence
Notice the first of the two consequences: the world must have been created ex nihilo. This undermines the materialist position.
This consequence directly contradicts the idea of an eternal world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول المفرق بين الحالين ظاهر التناقض ؛ لأنه أوجب أحد الوجهين لنفسه من التباين أو الاجتماع ؛ إذ ذلك وصفه بالقدم ، ثم ذهب عنه ذلك من غير ذهاب نفسه ، فبطل ما كان عليه السبب الذي به كان ، وذلك وجود علة ايجاد الشيء في حال ارتفاعه ، وذلك فاسد في العقل لو جاز ذا الجاز أن يصير القديم حديثا والحديث قديما ، وفى ذلك بطلان ما لو جاز وجود ما ثبت بنفسه زائلا وما زال بنفسه ثابتا الجاز مجود ميع ما قولهم في القدم ، مع ما وجد بنفسه عديما وعدم ما عدم بنفسه موجودا وفى ذلك وجهان : أحدهما كون العالم بعد أن لم يكن ووجوده بعد العدم ، وفى ذلك فساد مذهبهم ووجوب القول بحدث العالم بلا أصل له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى لو جاز أن يصير المجتمع بذاته متفرقا ، والمتفرق بذاته مجتمعا من غير حدث به الجاز كون المجتمع متفرقا وقت كونه مجتمعا ؛ إذ ذاته قائم ، وذلك مما لا صبر للعقل عليه ، مع ما يزول به معرفة الأغيار البتة ؛ إذ لا على عليه عليه أدل الذي ذكرت ، وفى ذلك جواز جعل الشر خيرا والظلمة نورا والحى ميتا والمتحرك ساكنا والبارد خارا ونحو ذلك من الأضداد ، وفى جواز ذلك بطلان القول بقدم التباين والاجتماع ؛ إذ كانا معا، وفى ذلك فساد القول بالدهر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the saying of the one who distinguishes between the two states is manifestly contradictory; because he necessitates one of the two aspects for itself from separation or combination, since that describes it with pre-eternity, then it departs from that without its self departing, so the cause by which it was becomes invalid, and that is the existence of the cause of bringing a thing into existence in the state of its removal, and that is rationally corrupt. If it were permissible, then it would be permissible for the pre-eternal to become temporal and the temporal to become pre-eternal. And in that is the invalidity that if it were permissible for what is established by itself to be perishing and what is perpetual by itself to be established, then it would be permissible for [?] their saying about pre-eternity. With what exists by itself being non-existent and what is non-existent by itself being existent, and in that are two aspects: one of them is the world's coming to be after it was not, and its existence after non-existence, and in that is the corruption of their doctrine and the necessity of holding the world's temporal origination without an origin thereof; and there is no power except in God. The second: if it were permissible for the combined by itself to become separate, and the separate by itself to become combined, without an incident occurring, it would be permissible for the combined to be separate at the time of its being combined, since its essence subsists, and that is something the intellect cannot bear, along with which the knowledge of other things would be utterly removed, for there is no [?] evidence like what I mentioned. And in that is the permissibility of making evil good, darkness light, the living dead, the moving still, the cold hot, and similar opposites. And if that is permissible, then the statement about the pre-eternity of separation and combination is invalid, since they would be together; and in that is the corruption of the doctrine of the Dahriyya. And there is no power except in God.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the view that distinguishes between two eternally opposite states (separation and combination) for the world. Maturidi begins by stating that such a view is manifestly contradictory. The argument proceeds as follows: The one who separates the two states necessarily ascribes one of them (either separation or combination) to the thing as a description of its pre-eternity. Then that state supposedly departs from the thing without the thing itself departing. This results in the absurdity that the cause of the thing's existence would exist in the state of its removal, which is rationally corrupt. If this were permissible, it would also be permissible for the pre-eternal to become temporal and the temporal to become pre-eternal, which is impossible. From this absurdity, two consequences follow: First, the world must have come into existence after non-existence, meaning it is temporally originated (ḥādith) and has no pre-eternal origin, which nullifies the materialists' doctrine. Second, if it were permissible for a thing to change from one state to its opposite without an external cause (ḥadath), then a thing could be both combined and separate at the same time, since its essence remains. The intellect cannot accept this, and it would destroy all knowledge of distinct things, allowing opposites to become each other (e.g., evil becoming good, darkness becoming light). Since this is absurd, the doctrine of the pre-eternity of both separation and combination is invalid, and thus the entire materialist (dahrī) position collapses. The passage ends with a formula acknowledging divine power.

Key Terms
المفرق بين الحالين
القدم
الحدث
الوجهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is merely logical trickery without real implications.
Correction: Maturidi shows that attributing change to an eternal entity leads to rational absurdities that have real theological consequences, such as the necessity of the world's temporal origination.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the two consequences Maturidi derives from the absurdity of an eternal thing changing states?
A) The world must be pre-eternal
B) The world must have been created ex nihilo
C) The world must have an eternal origin
D) The world is composed of atoms

Key Takeaway
An eternal thing cannot change its fundamental state without contradiction, so the world must be temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God alone is eternal and unchanging. Yet, do you ever find yourself expecting God to change His decree or respond to your whims? How does this lesson's argument that an eternal thing cannot depart from its state expose the inconsistency in such expectations? What would it take for you to submit fully to His unchanging nature?
?
The lesson concludes that the world must be temporally originated—it has no pre-eternal origin. If you truly internalized that this world is a temporary creation with no eternal essence, what would you change about your daily priorities? Are you building your life on something that will perish, or on the One who alone is eternal?
?
Maturidi argues that attributing change to an eternal entity leads to absurdity. Consider your own spiritual state: do you treat your faith as a permanent, unchanging possession, assuming you will always be a believer without effort? How does this argument challenge your complacency and call you to actively renew your faith each day?

Refutation of Two Eternal States: Second Consequence and Refutation of Materialism
Maturidi derives the second absurd consequence and refutes the Dahriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absurdity of opposites collapsing further refutes the materialist (dahrī) doctrine.

Core Idea
If an eternal thing could change states, then all opposites could become each other, destroying all knowledge.

Maturidi continues by drawing out a second devastating consequence: if an eternal thing could change from one state to its opposite without an external cause, then a thing could be both separate and combined simultaneously. This would allow evil to become good, darkness to become light, and so on, which is rationally unacceptable. Consequently, the materialist doctrine of an eternal universe ('dahr') collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the condition: 'if it were permissible for the combined by itself to become separate...'
Notice the hypothetical nature of the argument.
2
Analyze the absurdity
Consider why the intellect cannot accept that a thing could be both combined and separate at the same time. What does that imply for the knowledge of other things?
3
See the final refutation
Maturidi concludes that such permissibility would allow opposites to become each other, thus invalidating the pre-eternity of separation/combination and the entire dahrī position.
This shows the materialist view leads to total irrationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول المفرق بين الحالين ظاهر التناقض ؛ لأنه أوجب أحد الوجهين لنفسه من التباين أو الاجتماع ؛ إذ ذلك وصفه بالقدم ، ثم ذهب عنه ذلك من غير ذهاب نفسه ، فبطل ما كان عليه السبب الذي به كان ، وذلك وجود علة ايجاد الشيء في حال ارتفاعه ، وذلك فاسد في العقل لو جاز ذا الجاز أن يصير القديم حديثا والحديث قديما ، وفى ذلك بطلان ما لو جاز وجود ما ثبت بنفسه زائلا وما زال بنفسه ثابتا الجاز مجود ميع ما قولهم في القدم ، مع ما وجد بنفسه عديما وعدم ما عدم بنفسه موجودا وفى ذلك وجهان : أحدهما كون العالم بعد أن لم يكن ووجوده بعد العدم ، وفى ذلك فساد مذهبهم ووجوب القول بحدث العالم بلا أصل له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى لو جاز أن يصير المجتمع بذاته متفرقا ، والمتفرق بذاته مجتمعا من غير حدث به الجاز كون المجتمع متفرقا وقت كونه مجتمعا ؛ إذ ذاته قائم ، وذلك مما لا صبر للعقل عليه ، مع ما يزول به معرفة الأغيار البتة ؛ إذ لا على عليه عليه أدل الذي ذكرت ، وفى ذلك جواز جعل الشر خيرا والظلمة نورا والحى ميتا والمتحرك ساكنا والبارد خارا ونحو ذلك من الأضداد ، وفى جواز ذلك بطلان القول بقدم التباين والاجتماع ؛ إذ كانا معا، وفى ذلك فساد القول بالدهر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the saying of the one who distinguishes between the two states is manifestly contradictory; because he necessitates one of the two aspects for itself from separation or combination, since that describes it with pre-eternity, then it departs from that without its self departing, so the cause by which it was becomes invalid, and that is the existence of the cause of bringing a thing into existence in the state of its removal, and that is rationally corrupt. If it were permissible, then it would be permissible for the pre-eternal to become temporal and the temporal to become pre-eternal. And in that is the invalidity that if it were permissible for what is established by itself to be perishing and what is perpetual by itself to be established, then it would be permissible for [?] their saying about pre-eternity. With what exists by itself being non-existent and what is non-existent by itself being existent, and in that are two aspects: one of them is the world's coming to be after it was not, and its existence after non-existence, and in that is the corruption of their doctrine and the necessity of holding the world's temporal origination without an origin thereof; and there is no power except in God. The second: if it were permissible for the combined by itself to become separate, and the separate by itself to become combined, without an incident occurring, it would be permissible for the combined to be separate at the time of its being combined, since its essence subsists, and that is something the intellect cannot bear, along with which the knowledge of other things would be utterly removed, for there is no [?] evidence like what I mentioned. And in that is the permissibility of making evil good, darkness light, the living dead, the moving still, the cold hot, and similar opposites. And if that is permissible, then the statement about the pre-eternity of separation and combination is invalid, since they would be together; and in that is the corruption of the doctrine of the Dahriyya. And there is no power except in God.

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the view that distinguishes between two eternally opposite states (separation and combination) for the world. Maturidi begins by stating that such a view is manifestly contradictory. The argument proceeds as follows: The one who separates the two states necessarily ascribes one of them (either separation or combination) to the thing as a description of its pre-eternity. Then that state supposedly departs from the thing without the thing itself departing. This results in the absurdity that the cause of the thing's existence would exist in the state of its removal, which is rationally corrupt. If this were permissible, it would also be permissible for the pre-eternal to become temporal and the temporal to become pre-eternal, which is impossible. From this absurdity, two consequences follow: First, the world must have come into existence after non-existence, meaning it is temporally originated (ḥādith) and has no pre-eternal origin, which nullifies the materialists' doctrine. Second, if it were permissible for a thing to change from one state to its opposite without an external cause (ḥadath), then a thing could be both combined and separate at the same time, since its essence remains. The intellect cannot accept this, and it would destroy all knowledge of distinct things, allowing opposites to become each other (e.g., evil becoming good, darkness becoming light). Since this is absurd, the doctrine of the pre-eternity of both separation and combination is invalid, and thus the entire materialist (dahrī) position collapses. The passage ends with a formula acknowledging divine power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The materialists only believed in an eternal world; Maturidi's argument is just a logical game.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the materialist position leads to the acceptance of contradictory states, which would destroy all rational knowledge, not just the concept of an eternal world.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say would follow from the permissibility of a combined thing becoming separate by itself?

B) Opposites could become each other

D) Knowledge of other things would be enhanced

Key Takeaway
The materialist doctrine collapses because it leads to the absurd conclusion that opposites can coexist and interchange.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that good and evil are distinct, objective realities. But do you live as if those distinctions could shift based on your convenience? What would it mean for your soul if you truly believed that 'evil becoming good' is impossible?
?
The intellect refuses to accept that an eternal thing could be both combined and separate at the same time. Yet in your own life, do you try to hold contradictory beliefs or actions without confronting the inconsistency? Where are you refusing the clarity that reason demands?
?
Maturidi says that if opposites could interchange, 'knowledge of other things would be utterly removed.' Is there an area of your faith where you have allowed confusion to replace clear knowledge? What truth are you avoiding because it would require you to reject a comfortable falsehood?

Two Faces of Argument for the World's Createdness
Maturidi presents two arguments refuting eternalism and proving the world's createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two rational arguments (faces) that refute the eternity of the world and establish its temporality.

Core Idea
Two rational arguments show the world must be temporal, not eternal.

Imam Maturidi presents two interconnected arguments refuting the Dahriyya (eternalists). He first lays a principle about existence and nonexistence, then gives two faces: the world's coming into being after nonexistence, and the absurdity of self-caused change. Both undermine the claim of an eternal world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to capture the flow of Maturidi's reasoning.
Pay attention to the initial paradoxical statement.
2
Understand the initial paradox
Reflect on the opening line: 'What exists by itself is nonexistent, and what is nonexistent by itself is existent.' This sets up the need for a cause.
3
Examine the first face
The first face argues that the world came into being after not existing. This directly refutes the eternalist claim and proves the world is temporal.
This is a straightforward argument from contingency.
4
Examine the second face
The second face argues against the possibility of things changing their state (composite/separate) by themselves. If allowed, contradictions would follow, invalidating the eternity of such states.
This leads to absurdity and thus refutes the eternalist doctrine.
5
Connect to the refutation of eternalism
See how both faces together undermine the view that the world (including the states of separation and composition) is eternal. Maturidi concludes that the doctrine of al-dahr (eternal time) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما وجد بنفسه عديما، وعدم ما عدم بنفسه موجودا، وفي ذلك وجهان: أحدهما كون العالم بعد أن لم يكن، ووجوده بعد العدم، وفي ذلك فساد مذهبهم ووجوب القول بحدث العالم بلا أصل له، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني: لو جاز أن يصير المجتمع بذاته متفرقا، والمتفرق بذاته مجتمعا من غير حدث به، لجاز كون المجتمع متفرقا وقت كونه مجتمعا؛ إذ ذاته قائم، وذلك مما لا صبر للعقل عليه، مع ما يزول به معرفة الأغيار البتة؛ إذ لا دليل على ذلك، وفي ذلك جواز جعل الشر خيرا والظلمة نورا والحى ميتا والمتحرك ساكنا والبارد حارا ونحو ذلك من الأضداد، وفي جواز ذلك بطلان القول بقدم التباين والاجتماع؛ إذ كانا معا، وفي ذلك فساد القول بالدهر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
What exists by itself is nonexistent, and what is nonexistent by itself is existent. In this there are two faces: one is that the world came to be after it had not been, and its existence after nonexistence – in that is the corruption of their doctrine and the necessity of saying the world is temporal without any origin for it, and no power except by Allah. The second: if it were permissible for the composite to become separate by itself, and the separate to become composite by itself without an event occurring to it, then it would be permissible for the composite to be separate at the time of being composite, since its essence is enduring. That is something the intellect cannot bear, along with the total loss of knowledge of things; for there is no proof for that. In that is the permissibility of making evil good, darkness light, the living dead, the moving still, the cold hot, and similar opposites. With the permissibility of that, the claim of the eternity of separation and composition is invalidated, since they would be together. In that is the corruption of the doctrine of time (al-dahr). And no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 721

Commentary
This passage presents a two-part argument (two faces, وجهان) refuting the eternalists (Dahriyya) who claim the world is eternal. Maturidi first states a principle: what exists by itself is nonexistent (i.e., it could not bring itself into existence), and what is nonexistent by itself is existent (paradoxical). Then he explains the first face: the world's coming into being after nonexistence necessitates its temporality and refutes the eternalists. The second face argues against the possibility that things could change their state of composition or separation by themselves without a cause. If that were possible, a thing could be both composite and separate simultaneously, which is absurd. This absurdity leads to all opposites becoming interchangeable, invalidating the claim that separation and composition are eternal. Thus the doctrine of an eternal world (dahr) collapses. The passage ends with a reliance on divine power.

Key Terms
وجهان
حادث
الدهر
التباين
الاجتماع
الأضداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two faces are separate independent proofs for the world's createdness.
Correction: They are two aspects of a single refutation of eternalism, each undermining the claim of an eternal world.

Checkpoint
What does the second face argue against?
A) The existence of God
B) The possibility of change without a cause
C) The temporality of the world
D) The necessity of opposites

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's two faces rationally demonstrate the world's temporality, refuting the claim of an eternal universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if the world is a created, temporal reality sustained by Allah every moment—or do your daily worries and attachments betray a hidden assumption that this world is self-sufficient and eternal? What would change if you genuinely felt that nothing exists by itself but only through Allah?
?
The second argument shows that denying an external cause for change leads to absurdity—where opposites could become each other and all knowledge collapses. Where in your life do you secretly assume that you can change yourself or your circumstances without relying on Allah? How does that assumption subtly undermine your certainty in His power?
?
Maturidi says that if change could happen without an external cause, evil could become good and darkness light. Are there areas in your life where you have tried to redefine good and evil on your own terms, ignoring the reality that only Allah determines them? Will you today submit to His definitions even when they challenge your desires?

Allah's Existence
advanced
5 min
External Cause Leads to Tawhid and Refutes Dahriyya
Maturidi argues that composition/separation requires an external cause, establishing tawhid and refuting the eternalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the third possibility (external cause) to prove the temporality of composition/separation, establish the necessity of tawhid, and refute the eternalist (Dahri) position.

Core Idea
If composition needs an external cause, the world is temporal and must have a single Creator.

Maturidi has considered two possibilities for what causes composition and separation: nature or choice. Now he examines the third: an external cause. By showing that an external cause implies temporality, he demonstrates the necessity of a single Creator—which is exactly the tawhid the Dahriyya intended to deny. He then criticizes those who are ignorant of both nature and choice, showing they resort to blind imitation, and concludes that the Dahriyya's position collapses under the weight of its own contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its translation. Notice the flow: from discussing a cause 'by another' to concluding tawhid, then a critique of ignorance and a refutation of the Dahriyya.
Pay attention to the phrase 'تجهيل كل واحد منها الآخر وتعجيزه' which sets up the argument.
2
Understand the logical step from external cause to temporality
Maturidi argues that if composition/separation are due to an external cause (بآخر), then those states must be temporal (حادث) because they are brought into existence after not being. This temporality implies a need for a creator, which is the meaning of tawhid.
The phrase 'ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين' is the key: the temporality of separation and differentiation is established.
3
Examine the critique of those who are ignorant
Maturidi says whoever is ignorant of both earlier possibilities (nature and choice) admits they have no rational account and must rely on taqlid (imitation). This warns against neglecting rational inquiry into theological matters.
Note the contrast between 'عقله' (intellect) and 'تقليد' (imitation).
4
See the final refutation of the Dahriyya
The Dahriyya's two options (nature or choice) are contradictory as shown earlier. Since both have been shown corrupt, their entire doctrine is false. The truth must be the one that leads to tawhid.
Maturidi asserts that if there were any truth in their position, it would be in one of those two statements, but both are corrupt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن في تحقيق ذلك تجهيل كل واحد منها الآخر وتعجيزه، وفي ذلك إفساد القول، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن كان ذلك بآخر ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين، وهما لا يخلوان منه، حدثهما، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالتوحيد بما أريد به نفيه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ومن جهل الأمرين جميعا فقد أقر أن لا قول تكلم عليه، لا يحتمل عقله البلاغ إلى العلم به، وإنما طريقه التقليد، فأشكل عليه لاختلاف ما أدى إليه، فإنما تكلم من عنده أن الذي أداه إليه حق يظهر عند ذلك الحق، ثم إذ محال اجتماع الأمرين من حيث بينا من التناقض، فثبت أن الحق لو كان فما يقول أهل الدهر فهو في أحد ذينك القولين، وقد بينا فسادهما جميعا، وبالله المعونة.
And moreover, in the verification of that is the ignorance of each one of them of the other and its incapacitation, and in that is corruption of the statement, and no power except by Allah. And if that were by another, then the temporality of separation and differentiation is established, and they are not free from it, their temporality. In that is the necessity of saying tawheed (the oneness of God) by what was intended to negate it, and no power except by Allah. And whoever is ignorant of both matters has admitted that there is no statement to be spoken about it, his intellect cannot attain knowledge of it, and his only way is taqlid (imitation); so it becomes problematic for him due to the difference of what leads him. Then he who speaks from himself that what led him is the truth appears upon that truth. Then, since the conjunction of the two matters is impossible from the perspective we have shown of contradiction, it is established that if there were any truth in what the people of time (Dahriyya) say, it would be in one of those two statements, and we have shown the corruption of both. And Allah's assistance is sought.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage examines the third possibility: that composition/separation are caused by an external agent (بآخر). Maturidi argues that if there is an external cause, then the states of separation and composition must be temporal (hadith), arising after not being. This forces the admission of a creator, which is the very meaning of tawheed that the Dahriyya intended to deny. He then criticizes those who are ignorant of both possibilities (nature and choice) – they resort to blind imitation (taqlid) and cannot reach certainty. The Dahriyya are left with two contradictory options, both of which have been shown corrupt. Thus the truth is that the world is temporal and has a creator, establishing tawheed.

Key Terms
آخر
حادث
توحيد
تقليد
الدهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Maturidi explicitly identifies the 'external cause' as Allah in this passage.
Correction: The passage does not name the external cause; rather, it argues that the temporality resulting from an external cause necessitates the existence of a Creator, which is the meaning of tawhid. The argument leads to tawhid but does not state 'God is the cause' directly here.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the third possibility (by another)?
A) The world is eternal
B) Tawheed is necessary
C) Choice is impossible
D) Nature explains everything

Key Takeaway
The third possibility (external cause) leads to the necessity of tawhid and refutes the eternalists' position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the very composition and separation in the world around you—from the splitting of atoms to the diversity of species—forces the conclusion that there is one Creator. If this is undeniably true, then why do you still live as if the world runs on its own? Will you let this certainty reshape how you see every detail of your existence starting now?
?
The passage warns that those who rely on blind imitation (taqlid) remain confused about the ultimate cause. Where in your own faith have you accepted beliefs without personal verification? What is one area of your aqeedah that you have never truly investigated, and what steps will you take today to move from imitation to conviction?
?
Consider the Dahriyya: they denied a Creator because they could not accept a cause outside the world. In what subtle ways do you deny Allah's constant involvement in your life—by attributing your successes to your own effort or your failures to circumstances? If every moment of your existence is upheld by the One, what will you do tomorrow to consciously depend on Him?

The Impossibility of Contradiction Proves Tawhid
Maturidi builds a logical proof for tawhid from the impossibility of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the impossibility of logical contradiction to argue for the necessity of tawhid (divine unity).

Core Idea
If two contradictory claims cannot both be true, truth must be one — and that is tawhid.

Maturidi presents a logical argument that strikes at the heart of dualist and atheist claims. He shows that any pair of contradictory statements cannot both be correct; one must be false. By examining the implications of this principle, he demonstrates that truth is unitary, which leads to affirming the oneness of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
The passage contains some uncertain words due to OCR; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify key terms and concepts
Notice terms like الجهل (ignorance), العجز (incapacity), التناقض (contradiction). Maturidi uses these to frame the debate between two opposing sides.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument: if both sides are ignorant/incapable, their disagreement is invalid. If each declares the other ignorant, discourse corrupts. Separation implies a beginning, necessitating tawhid. Ignorance of both leads to taqlid, but contradiction cannot hold, so truth must be one.
This is the core logical chain.
4
Reflect on the conclusion against the people of al-dahr
Maturidi concludes that the dualists' position is one of two corrupt statements. Truth, if it exists, is unitary — affirming tawhid.
Al-dahr refers to materialists or atheists who deny the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختيار وتحقق فيها جميعا العجز والجهل ، وإن كان ذلك كذلك بطل الاختلاف عما كانا عليها ، لما به يصل كل إلى ما يؤديه ويضره . وبعد ، فإن [في؟] تحقيق ذلك تجهيل كل واحد منها الآخر وتعجيز [؟] ، وفى ذلك إفساد القول ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وإن كان ذلك بآخر ثبت حدث التفرق والتباين ، وهما لا يخلوان منه ، حدثهما ، وفى ذلك لزوم القول بالتوحيد بما أريد به نفيه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأنه ممن [قلزم؟] . ومن جهل الأمرين جميعا فقد أقر أن لا قول تكلم عليه ، لا يحتمل عقله البلاغ إلى العلم به ، وإنما طريقه التقليد ، فأشكل عليه لاختلاف ما أدى إليه ، فإنما تكلم من عنده أن الذي أداه إليه حق يظهر عند ذلك الحق ، ثم إذ محال اجتماع الأمرين من حيث بينا من التناقض ، فثبت أن الحق لو كان فما يقول أهل الدهر فهو فى أحد ذينك القولين ، وقد بينا فسادهما جميعا ، وبالله المعونة
Choice and realization in all of them of incapacity and ignorance. And if that is so, the disagreement about what they were upon becomes invalid, because by it each reaches what benefits and harms him. Then, in the investigation of that, there is a declaration of ignorance of each one of them of the other and a declaration of incapacity, and in that is corruption of speech, and there is no power except with Allah. And if that is by another, the occurrence of separation and divergence is established, and they are not free from it, their occurrence, and in that is the necessity of adhering to tawhid by what is intended to negate it, and there is no power except with Allah. And he is among those who [؟]. And whoever is ignorant of both matters has admitted that there is no speech spoken upon it, his intellect cannot attain knowledge of it, and his way is only taqlid, so it becomes difficult for him due to the disagreement of what led him. For he only speaks from himself that what led him is truth that appears at that truth. Then, since it is impossible for the two matters to be together from the aspect of contradiction we have shown, it is established that if truth exists, what the people of al-dahr say is in one of those two statements, and we have shown the corruption of both of them, and with Allah is assistance.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the impossibility of contradiction to the necessity of tawhid (divine unity). The author begins by stating that if both opposing parties are characterized by ignorance and incapacity, then their disagreement is invalid because each would only follow what benefits or harms them. He then notes that if each side declares the other ignorant and incapable, the entire discourse is corrupted. He further argues that separation and divergence (tafarruq and tabayun) cannot exist without having a beginning, which implies the necessity of affirming tawhid as the negation of duality. The passage then considers someone who is ignorant of both sides; such a person admits he has no definitive statement, his intellect cannot reach knowledge, so he resorts to taqlid (blind following) and is confused by conflicting opinions. If he then claims that what led him is the truth, it is impossible for two contradictory statements to both be true. Therefore, if truth exists, the position of the dualists (ahl al-dahr) must be one of the two statements, both of which have been shown to be corrupt.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التضاد
التناقض
الجهل
العجز
أهل الدهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two contradictory statements can both be true in different contexts or perspectives.
Correction: This passage argues that it is impossible (muhal) for two contradictory claims to coexist as true. If one is true, the other must be false, regardless of context. This principle leads to affirming the unity of truth, i.e., tawhid.

Checkpoint

A) That both statements may be true in different contexts
B) That truth must be one, which is tawhid
C) That human intellect cannot know truth
D) That taqlid is the only way to truth

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of contradiction requires that truth be one, which is the foundation of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The proof says separation must have a beginning—everything dual eventually traces back to one cause. Is there an area of your life where you act as if there are two competing loyalties (e.g., pleasing Allah and pleasing people)? What concrete step will you take to unify your heart under tawhid?

Ibn Shabib's Proof from Contradiction
Maturidi quotes Ibn Shabib's argument that contradiction proves the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muhammad ibn Shabib uses the presence of opposition and contradiction in the world to argue that it must have been created by an external agent.

Core Idea
Contradiction in the world proves it cannot be eternal; a Creator must have brought it into existence.

Maturidi presents the reasoning of the Mu'tazili scholar Muhammad ibn Shabib, who argues that the very existence of opposition and contradiction in the world shows that the world cannot be self-subsisting or eternal. Instead, it must have been created by an external agent who prevents its dissolution due to inner contradictions. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the dense logical structure and the key terms like 'opposition' (التضاد) and 'contradiction' (التناقض).
The translation is literal; some phrases may seem awkward but capture the original reasoning.
2
Identify the core claim
Ibn Shabib claims that something that contains opposition and contradiction cannot exist eternally on its own. Why? Because contradiction by nature leads to dissolution. Identify this premise in the text.
3
Examine the analogy of the house
The analogy of the house with a conditional entry illustrates how a condition can make existence impossible. Understand the comparison: just as the condition prevents entry, inherent contradiction prevents eternal existence unless an external 'preventer' (māni‘) allows it.
4
Consider the two alternatives and their refutation
Ibn Shabib presents two possibilities for a thing with inherent contradiction: either it remains eternal (impossible due to contradiction) or it chooses annihilation (possible if it has free will). Both are shown invalid, proving it must have been created.
The refutation of the second alternative relies on the idea that if it could choose annihilation, it would, but it persists, so it cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال محمد بن شبيب فى ذلك بما كان معناه عندنا إنه إذ لا يخلو القائم على ما عليه من التضاد والتناقض من أن يكون كذلك أبدا ، فيبطل كونه ، من حيث ذلك فيكون لا يتوهم ، مع كون شيء من الجملة إلا أن يكون شيء هو فيها كل كائن منها المانع لكونه ، فيبطل ، كمن يقول : لا يدخل أحد هذه الدار حتى يدخلها غيره ، إنها لا تحتمل دخول أحد فيها على وفاء الشرط ، أو إن كان عن تباين قد تقدم ، فيبطل الوجود للتضاد ؛ إذ حقه التنافر بما تضادا بالطبع ، ولو احتمل الخروج عن طبعها الذي فيه التضاد والتضاد يوجب ما ذكرت - بالاختيار الجاز اختيار الفناء له فى نفسه ، وإن كان هو بطبعه باق . وإذا بطل الوجهان ثبت أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن بمن أحدثه كذلك على ما فيه الاختلاف والاتفاق ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Muhammad ibn Shabib said regarding that, in what its meaning with us is: that since that which subsists upon what it has of opposition and contradiction is not free from being so always, its existence becomes invalid from that aspect, so it cannot be imagined, with the existence of something of the whole except that there is something in it that is the preventer of its existence, so it is invalid. Like one who says: No one enters this house until someone else enters it; it does not allow anyone to enter it fulfilling the condition. Or if it is due to a prior divergence, then existence is invalid due to opposition, for its due is repulsion by what opposes by nature. And if it could deviate from its nature which contains opposition – and opposition necessitates what I mentioned – by choice, it would be permissible to choose annihilation for itself, even if it is by nature everlasting. And since both aspects are invalid, it is established that it came into existence after not having been, by one who brought it into existence, thus, upon what it has of difference and agreement. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 722

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of Muhammad ibn Shabib, a Mu'tazili scholar, as cited by Maturidi. It is a proof that the world cannot be eternal because it contains opposition and contradiction. The argument proceeds as follows: If something subsists (qā'im) with opposition and contradiction within it always, then its existence would be invalid because contradiction inherently destroys existence. However, we observe that the world exists despite containing opposites. Therefore, there must be a preventer (māni') that allows its existence despite the contradiction, namely a creator who brings it into existence after non-existence. Ibn Shabib uses an analogy: if someone says 'no one enters this house until someone else enters it', the condition makes entry impossible. Similarly, if existence were inherently contradictory, it could not persist unless it deviates from its nature by an external choice (ikhtiyār). If it could choose to annihilate itself, it could, but it remains, so it cannot be eternal. Since both the possibility of eternal existence with contradiction and the possibility of self-annihilation are invalid, the world must have been created by an external agent.

Key Terms
محمد بن شبيب
التضاد
التناقض
الاختيار
الفناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument only applies to material things, but it is a logical argument about the impossibility of inherent contradiction in any existing thing.
Correction: Ibn Shabib's argument is not about physical composition but about the logical necessity that contradiction cannot be sustained eternally; it applies to any entity, material or immaterial.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Shabib's analogy of the house illustrate?
A) That conditions can prevent entry
B) That inherent contradiction prevents existence without an external agent
C) That eternity is impossible for anything
D) That free will can annihilate the world

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib's argument demonstrates that the presence of contradiction in the world necessitates a Creator who brings it into existence, as inherent contradiction cannot sustain itself eternally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see contradiction every day—hope and despair, faith and doubt, life and decay. If this very contradiction proves the world cannot sustain itself, why do you still try to sustain your own life as if you were self-sufficient? What would change if you truly lived knowing that your existence is held together by a Creator at every moment?
?
Ibn Shabib says that if the world could choose, it would have annihilated itself. You have moments when you wish to escape, to disappear. Yet you are still here. Who is it that keeps you in existence despite your own desires for annihilation? How will you acknowledge that preserver today?

Three Proofs for the Necessity of a Creator
Maturidi gives three arguments for a creator from non-existence, form, and change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three concise arguments from Imam Maturidi that demonstrate the necessity of a creator.

Core Idea
Three simple observations point to an undeniable creator: existence, form, and change.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents three tightly reasoned proofs that the world cannot be self-caused. Each argument starts from an obvious feature of our experience—the difference between non-existence and existence, the presence of forms, and the changing seasons—and concludes that a creator must exist. Let's examine these proofs one by one.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the concise structure: each proof is introduced by 'li-anna' (because).
2
First proof: existence from non-existence
Reflect on the first argument: non-existence and existence are not the same. Something cannot bring itself from non-existence into existence. Therefore, a creator is needed.
This is a foundational argument in kalam for the contingency of the world.
3
Second proof: form requires a form-giver
Consider the second argument: every form (like the shape of a body) must have a giver of form. Since the world has forms, a form-giver (Allah) must exist.
4
Third proof: change of seasons
Think about the third argument: the change of seasons from winter to summer and similar changes require a changer. This proves the world is not eternal but created.
Change implies temporality, and temporality requires a creator.
5
Conclusion: 'it was like that'
Understand the phrase 'thabata annahu kāna ka-dhālika' – it establishes that the world was created as such, not that it always existed.
This phrase has been misunderstood by some as affirming eternity; but in context it means creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يجوز أن يحدث بلا محدث ; لما لا يكون العدم به والوجود إلا واحدا ، ولما لا يعرف صورة إلا من مصور ، ولما تغير الأوقات من شتاء وصيف ونحو ذلك ثبت أنه كان كذلك
Then it is not permissible for something to come into existence without a bringer into existence, because non-existence and existence are not one and the same, and because no form is known except from a form-giver, and because of the change of seasons from winter to summer and the like, it is established that it was like that.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage contains three concise proofs for the necessity of a creator (muḥdith). The first proof: non-existence and existence are not identical; something cannot transition from non-existence to existence by itself, so a creator is needed. The second proof: every form (ṣūra) requires a form-giver (muṣawwir). The third proof: the change of seasons (e.g., winter to summer) indicates a changer, proving the world is not eternal but came into existence (kāna ka-dhālika, 'it was like that' meaning it was created). The phrase 'it was like that' refers back to the earlier argument that the world was created.

Key Terms
محدث
مصور
تغير الأوقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'it was like that' might be interpreted as affirming the world's eternity.
Correction: In context, 'kāna ka-dhālika' refers back to the preceding arguments that the world is created, not eternal. It means 'it was created as such.'

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three proofs for a creator mentioned in this passage?
A) Non-existence and existence are not one
B) Every form needs a form-giver
C) The order of the cosmos
D) The change of seasons

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that three undeniable features of the world—the fact of coming into existence, the existence of form, and the change of seasons—all require a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you could not have brought yourself into existence, or do you subtly live as if your life is self-made? What would change if you fully internalized that your very existence depends on a Creator every moment?
?
When you look at your own body—its form, its design—do you ever stop and say, 'This was made by a Form-giver,' or do you take it for granted? Write down one feature of your body and thank the One who shaped it.
?
The change of seasons—winter to summer—is a constant reminder of a Changer. How often do you let the weather point you to your Lord? Starting today, will you make the changing seasons a trigger for your gratitude and reflection?

Objection and Answer on the Timing of Creation
Maturidi refutes an objection about the timing of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that creation at a specific time implies divine need or arbitrariness.

Core Idea
Timing of creation reflects divine wisdom and benefit, not need.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a sophisticated objection: if God created the world at a specific moment, why not earlier or later? He presents an opponent's answer based on benefit (maslaha), then demonstrates its insufficiency through a reductio. This lesson unpacks the logic of both the objection and Maturidi's decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its rhythm. Note the key terms: المصلحة, التدبير, الوقت.
Focus on the objection marker فعورض and the answer marker فزعم.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objector's reasoning: if a self-existing thing cannot choose its time, why should creation by another be any different?
The objection tries to equate the temporal determination of creation with necessity.
3
Analyze the opponent's answer
The opponent claims that for things existing by another, their timing is managed for a benefit in religion or this world. This is supposed to distinguish the two cases.
This answer appeals to divine wisdom, but Maturidi finds it problematic.
4
Follow Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi argues that if the opponent's principle is accepted, it would force an absurd conclusion: that the first creation cannot be changed, and if it could be otherwise without benefit, the objection's reasoning collapses.
The refutation shows that the opponent's position is self-defeating.
5
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why Maturidi insists that divine actions are not bound by the same logic as contingent things. The key is divine transcendence and wisdom.
This connects to broader kalam discussions about God's freedom and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعورض بما لو كان فيما كان بنفسه يمنعه عن ذلك كونه في وقت دون وقت ، لم لا كان كذلك فيما كان بغيره ؟ فزعم أنه إذا كان بغيره تدبير كونه له لمصلحة فى الدين أو الدنيا ، وفيما كان لا بغيره ليس كذلك ، لذلك اختلف الأمران ، وهذا الذي يزعم يوجب أنه لا يجوز أن يجعل أول الخلق غير المستحن حتى يكون : في الذي ذكرنا ، وإذا جاز غيره بلا مصلحة لذلك الوقت دون غيره لا معنى لما قال
And it was objected: if what was by itself prevented it from being at one time without another, why is it not likewise with what is by another? Then he claimed that if it is by another, its existence is managed for it for a benefit (maslaha) in religion or worldly life, and what is not by another is not so; therefore the two matters differ. And this claim necessitates that it is not permissible to make the first of creation other than the [؟] until it is in what we mentioned; and if other than it is permitted without benefit for that time without another, what he said has no meaning.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector says: If a self-existing thing is prevented from existing at a certain time due to its own nature, why would a thing existing by another be different? The answer (attributed to an unnamed opponent) is that when something exists by another, its existence is managed for a benefit (maslaha) in religion or worldly life, whereas a self-existing thing has no such management. Thus, the two cases differ. However, Maturidi then refutes this answer: this claim would imply that the first creation cannot be other than what it is, and if it could be otherwise without benefit for that particular time, then the objector's argument is meaningless. The exact phrase 'غير المستحن' is unclear but likely refers to 'the first creation'.

Key Terms
المصلحة
التدبير
الوقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because time is eternal
D) Because creation cannot be at any time

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the opponent's answer by showing it leads to a contradiction: either the first creation is necessary or the benefit-based explanation collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Maturidi's Answer to the Objection of the First Creation
Maturidi answers the objection of the first creation's lack of benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that the first creation had no benefit, using the relativity of the concept of 'first'.

Core Idea
There is no absolute 'first' creation to object about.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts a subtle objection: if creation is for benefit, the very first creation had no one to benefit, so it seems pointless. His answer is elegant—he denies an absolute 'first', deflating the objection before it takes hold. Let us examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection closely
Read the first part of the Arabic: 'فعورض بأول خلق خلقه لنفسه، وليس ثمة مصلحة'. The objector claims the first creation was for God alone, with no benefit to any creature. This is meant to undermine the principle that creation is for benefit.
Notice the word 'مصلحة' (benefit) – the core of the debate.
2
Study Maturidi's brief answer
Read the rest: 'فزعم أنه ليس ثمة أول، وهو أصلح وقت ليقال فيه: لم لا خلق قبله؟ وإنما ذلك متى يكون هو'. Maturidi asserts there is no 'first' in an absolute sense. The question 'why not create before it?' only makes sense if there is a before. Since there is no before, the objection collapses.
The text ends abruptly; the answer is cut off but the key point is clear.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider: If there is no absolute temporal 'first', then every creation has a context. The objection presupposes a linear timeline with a starting point. Maturidi challenges that presupposition.
This connects to deeper discussions about time and creation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعورض بأول خلق خلقه لنفسه ، وليس ثمة مصلحة ، فزعم أنه ليس ثمة أول ، وهو أصلح وقت ليقال فيه : لم لا خلق قبله ؟ وإنما ذلك متى يكون هو
And it was objected by the first creation He created for Himself, and there is no benefit there. So he claimed that there is no 'first', and it is the most appropriate time to say about it: Why did He not create before it? And that is only when it is.
Source pages p. 723

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and a brief answer. The objector brings up the first creation (awwal khalq) that God created for Himself, implying that if creation is for a benefit, the first creation had no benefit since there was no creature to benefit. The answer (alleged) is that there is no 'first' creation in an absolute sense; the question 'Why did He not create before it?' is only meaningful when there is a 'before'. The phrase 'and it is the most appropriate time' suggests that the objection misses the point because the concept of a 'first' creation is relative. The passage is cut off but indicates Maturidi's response that the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
أول الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is unanswerable, implying creation for benefit is not a consistent principle.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the objection fails because it assumes an absolute first creation, which does not exist in the way the objector imagines.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's claim about the first creation?
A) It was created for a benefit
B) It was created for no benefit and thus the principle of al-aslah fails
C) It was created before time
D) It was created by itself

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's answer to the first creation objection is that there is no absolute 'first' creation, so the question of benefit does not arise.

God's Eternal Power and Temporal Creation
Maturidi answers the objection about eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between two meanings of 'God always creates' and why only one is doctrinally sound.

Core Idea
God is eternally creative, but each creation is temporal.

An objector might ask: if God is eternally powerful, why didn't He create before this creation? Maturidi answers by clarifying what 'always creating' could mean. Read his precise distinction to see how he preserves God's eternal power without making creation eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection and Maturidi's two-part answer.
The passage refers back to an earlier discussion on time.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find where Maturidi says 'If by your saying ... you mean ...' and 'And if by it you mean ...'. Notice the different conclusions.
The first interpretation is rejected, the second accepted.
3
Reflect on the core distinction
Ask yourself: why does 'always creating' entail pre-eternity of creatures in the first sense? And how does the second sense avoid that problem?
This distinction safeguards both God's eternal creative power and the temporality of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسؤال القدرة على خلق شيء قبل هذا الخلق يخرج على ما بيناه في الوقت والله على كل شيء قدير. ثم عورض بما لم لا كان لم يزل يحدث الأشياء؟ فأجاب بالذي تقدم ذكره من فساد كون شيء قبل شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: وجواب هذا عندنا أن يقال: لو أردت بقولك لم يزل يحدث الأشياء ليكون هي لم يزل، فذلك محال؛ لما فيه إثبات قدمها، وفي قدمها فساد إحداثها. وإن أردت به الإحداث ليكون كل شيء من ذلك لوقت كونه فذلك حق؛ إذ هو بذاته خالق، لا بغيره.
The question about the power to create something before this creation comes out based on what we explained about time, and God is powerful over everything. Then He was objected to: 'Why was He not always creating things?' So He answered with what was previously mentioned about the corruption of something before something ad infinitum. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The answer to this with us is that it is said: If by your saying 'He was always creating things' you mean that they were always existent, that is impossible because it entails their pre-eternity, and in their pre-eternity is the corruption of their origination. And if by it you mean origination such that everything is at the time of its existence, that is true because He is in His essence a Creator, not through another.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage addresses two related objections. First, someone might ask why God did not create something before this creation. Maturidi refers back to the earlier discussion on time. Then a stronger objection follows: why was God not always creating things? This implies an infinite series of creations. Maturidi's answer distinguishes two interpretations: If by 'always creating' one means that the created things themselves are eternal, that is impossible because it would make creation eternal, contradicting the very concept of origination. If one means that God is eternally the Creator in the sense that He brings things into existence at their appointed times, that is true, because God is Creator by His essence, not by acquisition. This defends the orthodox view that God is eternally Creator but creation is temporal.

Key Terms
لم يزل
القدم
الإحداث
خالق بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal power means that the things He creates must be eternal.
Correction: God's power is eternal, but His act of creation is temporal; each thing comes into existence at its appointed time.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with saying 'God was always creating things' to mean that created things are eternal?
A) It is impossible because God cannot create.
B) It entails pre-eternity of creatures, which contradicts their being created.
C) It means God is not the Creator.
D) It is a valid interpretation.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal power does not require eternal creation; He creates each thing at its proper time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every moment of your existence is a fresh creation from God, or do you take your continued existence for granted? What would change in your daily awareness if you lived as if each second were a new act of divine origination?
?
When you pray for something and it does not come immediately, do you become anxious, assuming God has not heard? Reflect: does this impatience reveal a subtle disbelief in the doctrine that God creates each thing at its appointed time, not a moment earlier?
?
The passage says God is Creator by His essence, not through another. If God's power to create is inseparable from His very being, then you are never outside the reach of His creative action. Are you living in a way that acknowledges that He is constantly able to create new opportunities, new forgiveness, new guidance for you—if only you turn to Him?

Answering the Atheist's Question: What is the One Worshipped?
Maturidi refutes an atheist's question about the nature of the One worshipped.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi refutes the atheist's demand to define God by sensory or imaginative comparison, and why the only valid answer is 'Allah, the One, nothing like Him'.

Core Idea
God is known through proofs, not through senses or imagination.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to an objection from an atheist represented by Muhammad ibn Shabib. The atheist asks: 'What is this One that you worship?'—demanding a definition that would allow comparison to idols. Maturidi explains that we do not know God through the senses or through mental images, but through rational proofs and the testimony of the world. The proper answer is simply 'Allah, the One, nothing like Him,' and any further questioning based on imagination is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the atheist's question and Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the atheist's objection
The atheist asks 'What is it?' implying a demand for a definition that would allow comparison. Maturidi refutes this by showing we don't know Allah through senses or imagination.
3
Understand the basis of knowledge
Maturidi states we know Allah through proofs (adilla) and the testimony of the world (shahadat al-alam). This is the core epistemological point.
This guards against thinking God can be perceived or imagined.
4
See the conclusion and cutoff
Maturidi gives the answer 'Allah, the One, nothing like Him' and says we cut off further questioning because it relies on imagination, which cannot grasp Allah.
The lesson ends with a reminder that all power is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما عارض محمد بن شبيب من أسئلة الملحدين، فعارض عن الواحد الذي يعبده؟ ما هو؟ وقد بينا ما يجاب له، وهو زعم أن ذا يحتمل مثل ذا، وقد بينا أن لا شبيه له، و[لا] يحتمل ما يشار إليه، ولم نكن نعرفه بالحواس / فاشير / إليه، وما هو بمعنى يوجد بالأدلة وشهادة العالم. وما هو؟ ما اسمه؟ الله الرحمن الرحيم. وبهذا الحرف وجواب ذلك عندنا الله الواحد الذي ليس كمثله شيء، نقطع نبيل العود إلى السؤال؛ لأنه يعود إلى ما يتصور في الوهم، وفى هذا نفيه إلا من حيث الوجود بالأدلة، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then we mention what Muhammad ibn Shabib objected with from the questions of the atheists. He objected concerning the One whom he worships: 'What is it?' We have already shown what is answered to him, and he claims that this could be like that, and we have shown that there is no likeness to Him, and [it does not] allow what is pointed to, and we do not know Him through the senses / so it is pointed / to Him, and what He is is known by proofs and the testimony of the world. 'What is He? What is His name?' Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And with this letter, the answer with us is: Allah, the One, there is nothing like Him. We cut off the return to the question because it returns to what is imagined in the mind, and in this is His negation except from the aspect of existence through proofs. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage records an objection from Muhammad ibn Shabib, who was an early mutakallim. The atheist asks about the One worshipped: 'What is it?' The objection seems to imply that if we say 'Allah', we are like those who say 'that' in reference to an idol. Maturidi responds that we do not know God through senses but through proofs and the testimony of the world. The answer is: Allah, the One, there is nothing like Him. He then says we cut off the return to the question, meaning we stop the cycle of questioning, because any further question would rely on imagination (wahm). God is known only by existence through proofs, not by sensory or imaginative comparison.

Key Terms

الملحدين
الحواس
الأدلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be known through senses or compared to created things.
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that we know God only through proofs and the testimony of the world, not through senses or imagination. There is nothing like Him.

Checkpoint

A) Through the senses
B) Through proofs and the testimony of the world
C) Through direct vision
D) Through speculation without evidence

Key Takeaway
God is not known through senses or imagination, but through rational proofs and the testimony of creation; the only proper description is 'Allah, the One, nothing like Him'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson says that further questioning beyond proper answers leads to imagination, which negates Allah except by admitting His existence through proofs. Is there an area in your faith where you let your imagination dictate your understanding of Allah (e.g., in prayer, in His attributes)? Name one step you will take this week to rely more on revealed knowledge and rational proofs rather than imagination.

God Beyond Senses and Imagination
Maturidi records and answers an atheist's question about God's identity and name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the atheist's question about God's identity and how he asserts that God is known only through evidence.

Core Idea
God is not known by senses or imagination, only by evidence.

In this passage, Maturidi records a challenge from an atheist who asks: 'What is the One you worship? What is it?' The question assumes God can be described like other things. Maturidi's answer establishes the fundamental Islamic principle of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
The passage contains an atheist's question and Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the atheist's question
Find the question: 'What is the One whom you worship? What is it?' This question implies God is like other things.
The question is recorded from Muhammad ibn Shabib.
3
Understand Maturidi's answer
Maturidi answers that God has no likeness, cannot be pointed to, is not known by senses, and is known only through evidence and the testimony of the world.
He also mentions God's names: Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim.
4
Grasp the rationale for cutting off the question
Maturidi says the question must be cut off because it returns to what is imagined in the mind. God is affirmed only from the perspective of existence through evidence.
This protects divine transcendence from imaginative speculation.

And with this expression, the answer to that according to us is: Allah, the One, nothing is like Him. We cut off the return to the question because it returns to what is imagined in the mind, and in this is His negation except from the perspective of existence through evidence, and there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage records an atheist's question posed by Muhammad ibn Shabib: 'What is the One whom you worship? What is it?' The questioner implies that God might be like other things. Maturidi states that the proper answer is that God is the One of whom nothing is similar. The question is invalid because it assumes God can be pointed to or sensed, whereas He is known only through evidence. The question about His name is answered with 'Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim'. Maturidi then concludes that the question must be cut off because it leads to imagination, and only the existence through evidence is affirmed.

Why Did God Create? The Question of Benefit
Maturidi answers why God created, emphasizing benefit for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's creation is for the benefit of creatures, not out of any need in Himself.

Core Idea
God creates for our benefit, not His own.

This short passage from Maturidi tackles a fundamental question: why did God create? The answer reveals a key distinction: creation is for the sake of the creatures, not for any need in God. We'll explore the initial answer and the nuanced objection it raises, and reflect on what this means for our understanding of God's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation provided. Notice the question-and-answer format.
The passage includes a page marker '[ ٦٣ ب ]'.
2
Identify the two questions
The passage contains an initial question and a follow-up objection. Identify both and see how they build on each other.
The second question challenges the premise of the first answer.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the objection fails. What does this tell us about God's motivation for creation?
The key point is that creation is purely for the benefit of creatures, not for any need in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه لسؤال الله / غريب إنه خلق المنافع الخلق. وسئل إنه ليم خلق ؟ [ ٦٣ ب ] قال : لمنافع الخلق ، وليم خلق المنافع الخلق ؟ وأى حاجة كانت للخلق ولا خلق ،
And his answer to the question (Allah knows best) that He created the benefits for creation. And he was asked: 'Why did He create?' He said: 'For the benefits of creation.' [Then they asked:] 'And why did He create the benefits for creation? What need had creation when there was no creation?'
Source pages p. 725

Commentary
This brief passage addresses the question of purpose: 'Why did God create?' The initial answer is that He created for the benefit of creation. But a further objection arises: if creation didn't exist yet, why create benefits? What need was there? The text seems to cut off, but the implication is that the question is flawed because benefits are for the sake of the creatures, not for God. The deeper point is that benefits are created with the creatures, so the question assumes a prior need which does not apply.

Refuting the Sophist: God's Power and the Egg
Maturidi refutes a sophist objection about God putting the world in an egg

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes a sophist objection about God's power by analyzing the ambiguity in the question and distinguishing possible interpretations.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited by contradictory hypotheticals.

This passage presents a classic sophist challenge: if God is all-powerful, can He put the entire world into an egg? At first glance, this seems to imply a contradiction, but Maturidi shows that the question is ambiguous. By clarifying what is intended, he demonstrates that either the scenario is impossible due to contradiction, or God can indeed do it by altering sizes. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to see the sophist's question and the immediate answer highlighting a contradiction.
Notice the initial answer focuses on the logical inconsistency of making an egg wider than itself.
2
Understand the contradiction
Analyze why the literal scenario is impossible: if the egg remains its normal size and the world remains its size, then placing the world in the egg would require the egg to be larger—a contradiction.
The sophist's question appears to force a choice between God's power and logic.
3
Learn Maturidi's nuanced answer
Study the faqih's three interpretations: (1) keeping the egg unchanged and putting part of the world (impossible due to part-whole conflict), (2) keeping both unchanged (impossible as per Muhammad ibn Shabib), (3) changing sizes (possible).
Maturidi shows that the answer depends on the intended meaning; God can do what is not contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسأل عن الله أنه أليس على كل شيء قدير؟ فأجاب بنعم. يقدر على إدخال الدنيا في بيضة، فأجاب بالتناقض؛ لما في ذلك جعل البيضة أوسع منها، وقد جعلها أضيق منها؛ إذ هي جزء منها، وكذلك هذا التأويل في الأصغر والأكبر. قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجوابه عندنا أنه أراد بما قال على إبقاء البيضة بغضا للدنيا فهو محال؛ لما فيها انقلاب بعض كلاً، وكل بعضاً بلا تغير حاله، وذلك تناقض. وإن أراد بالبيضة وغير البيضة من الدنيا يجعل فيها فهو على وجهين: أحدهما أن يكونا بحالها، فقد أحال لما ذكر محمد بن شبيب. وإن أراد به بذلك تصغير ما قال أو توسيع البيض حتى يسع فيه ما وصف فهو على ذلك قادر، وبالله التوفيق.
He asked about God: 'Is He not powerful over all things?' He answered yes. 'He can put the world into an egg.' He answered with contradiction, because in that is making the egg wider than it, and He has made it narrower than it, since it is a part of it. And likewise this interpretation in the smaller and larger. The faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: Its answer with us is that what he said intends, upon keeping the egg, [making] part of the world, and it is impossible because in it is changing some into all and all into some without changing its state, and that is a contradiction. And if he intends by the egg and other than the egg from the world to be put into it, then it is two aspects: one of them is that they remain as they are, and it is impossible due to what Muhammad ibn Shabib mentioned. And if he intends thereby to make small what he said or to enlarge the egg until it contains what he described, then He is able to do that. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. A sophist asks whether God can put the entire world into an egg. The immediate answer highlights the contradiction: making the world fit into an egg would require the egg to be larger than itself or the world to be smaller, which is impossible if both remain unchanged. The faqih (Maturidi?) then gives a more nuanced answer: if the questioner intends to keep the egg as is and place part of the world in it, that involves a contradiction (part becoming whole). If he intends to put the world into the egg without changing the egg's size, that is impossible as per Muhammad ibn Shabib. But if he intends to shrink the world or enlarge the egg, then God can do that. The lesson is that questions about divine power must consider exact intent.

Key Terms
قدرة
بيضة
تناقض
إدخال الدنيا
محمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sophist's question shows that God cannot put the world into an egg, thus limiting His power.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that the apparent limit arises from an ambiguous question. When properly clarified, God's power is not diminished; He can alter sizes to make it happen.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, under which interpretation does God putting the world into an egg become possible?
A) If both the egg and the world remain unchanged.
B) If the egg is kept and only a part of the world is placed in it.
C) If God shrinks the world or enlarges the egg.
D) If the egg is considered a part of the world.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that apparent limits on God's power often stem from ambiguous or contradictory premises, not from any deficiency in divine ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological difficulty that seems like a contradiction, do you immediately assume God's power is limited, or do you pause to examine your own assumptions? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of hasty conclusions?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you felt God could not resolve a problem because it seemed impossible. How does Maturidi's method of clarifying ambiguous premises apply to your own doubts? Will you now seek to uncover hidden assumptions before despairing of God's power?
?
Are you ever like the sophist—posing intellectual objections to God's power not to seek truth but to avoid submitting to His actual commands? How can you turn this lesson into a genuine trust in God's unlimited ability, even when your mind cannot grasp it?
